You're naive.   They once (in the last three years, no less) had a single RMA expert working in that office. Everyone else in the RMA section of the company are telephone operators with scripts. If they wanted to improve their service, if they wanted to compete for your money, they would have that office fully staffed with RMA specialists and entry level RMA workers rather than outsourcing to a third party telephone answering service with scripts.   Secondly, they are in full control of product quality, not the end user. Currently they are foisting all product quality liability onto the customer. It's unacceptable... and totally avoidable since literally every other online retailer, including Wish (which is a hellhole in every other regard), takes the onus of replacement, repair, and repayment almost without question, which is naive on their part, but ultimately the most sustainable from the POV of maintaining customer satisfaction and market share. They don't care... because, as you pointed out, they've got a monopoly. Amazon is not their competition. If Amazon's abysmal selection and fake listings and bricks in packaging and repainted/refurbished/recycled parts from dodgy Asian sellers is direct competition with NewEgg, then... so is Wish.   They have no true competition in the US and they know it. They'll make nice for the cameras because you all dogpiled them (I'm not complaining, merely pointing it out) on social media and they felt the burn for a bit. Five months from now they'll return to complacency. And the internet will have all forgotten and wonder why we were ever angry with NewEgg. Profits will increase.   But the RMA division will still be outsourced, scripted, and employ exactly enough RMA specialists (or people who claim to be) to make their shareholders feel comfortable, but not enough for customers to have a pleasant experience, because that costs money.

I'm not a big fish by any stretch, but I'm the owner of a computer repair shop in Penticton, B.C.  Last year Newegg sold me a *counterfeit* nVidia GPU.  The label looked like it was printed on an inkjet, the logo was drawn wrong, the enclosed CD was a burnt disc with no label, and the installation sheet was photocopied.  Out of desperation (it was late and the customer wanted it for their son's birthday) I installed it.  Worked, albeit not great.  I tried installing the latest downloaded drivers and they refused to install.  Installed the *much* older drivers on the disc and it worked.  Gave the customer a hefty discount for the mix-up, but it doesn't matter because it stopped working two days later.  Newegg didn't want to return it.  I put them on blast and they finally agreed to take it back.  Well, that was the last thing I ordered from them, and unless I'm incredibly desperate I won't be ordering anything else from them.

We too grew up loving to buy form Newegg, so much when we introduced them to our employers, even when many of us departed these jobs those companies continued to buy Newegg because of the way they did business. When I departed the workforce to open my own company, I continued to purchase large amounts of products from Newegg for over the last 10 years. The first thing I started having issues with was shipping times being missed, we would pay next day air from Amazon and even though they were higher priced on many items, we have stopped purchasing from Newegg almost completely. Paying $20 or $30 dollars more on something for a critical project means nothing if you lose the job. EEAS Official: Human Rights Is a Must, not Optional  Dominic Porter from EEAS called in to the meeting online.  Dominic Porter from the European External Action Service (EEAS), an agency connected with the foreign and defense ministry of the EU, also called in to the meeting online. As head of the division responsible for China, Hong Kong, Macao, Taiwan, and Mongolia, he said the EEAS is seriously concerned about the organ harvesting in China.  Porter noted that the hearing attendees had heard many findings from various investigations, including disturbing allegations ranging from sexual abuse, torture, DNA testing, and illegal detention to organ harvesting in China. More and more evidence collected has shown that Falun Gong practitioners, minority ethnic groups, and faith groups in China are being persecuted in China.  Porter also said that the EU strongly condemned the crime of organ harvesting in China because it is inhumane and immoral. seems like this could be miscommunication between MSI and Newegg or a simple data entry mistake. It seems to me that it came in as unrepaired by the vendor but marked as repaired internally. Their procedure must be to not inspect boards returned from the vendors. It was then marked as an open box without an inspection. The proof is the MSI inspection sticker is left on the board. Why inspect a board leaving a sticker even if the goal was to resell a damaged motherboard? You wouldn't. You would take the sticker off. Then again, the RMA team inspected this board on Steve's return and likely saw the sticker. What the hell happened there? Why did they not immediately realize they shipped out a board to a customer that was returned by the vendor faulty? They should have refunded Steve the second they discovered the sticker and verified the RMA path the board previously took. So, poor training in the RMA department? Poor interdepartmental communication for things like special cases?  So a data entry mistake. There is an easy fix. Require all vendor repairs to be checked in-house through the same customer RMA process before sending to inventory. Verify the repair was done, done correctly, and no other underline issues exist. For instance, the socket was repaired but traces in the board were also damaged and the board is still not functional. If this simple step of sending the product back through the RMA process was done, the RMA department opens that box and sees a damaged board. They divert it to eWaste. that is assuming there is a way for someone in the RMA department to escalate the vendor RMA sticker finding through proper channels.  On the flip side, through a big vendor like MSI, something like a socket repair should have a known fixed price based on the socket type. Why would Newegg not already know what the repair cost was going to be before they sent it? Did they know because they were willing to pay $100 to repair a $500 board and it is MSI's mistake for not repairing it? Maybe Newegg did request the repair but the PR to pay MSI for the repair fell through the cracks and MSI shipped an unrepaired board back?   I don't think we know everything. It will be interesting what MSI reveals in the meeting.

I built my first PC with parts I was able to buy all at once, all from newegg back in 2011. It was an i5-2500k with a Radeon 6950 and 128GB SSD. Over the years I spent a few thousand more with them, over the years my experience gradually went down hill.  Most recently in 2021 I bought a Mobo, CPU and monitor from them. The CPU was defective, after a week troubleshooting it, I had to buy a newer and different one for $70 more at a local shop in a time crunch, Newegg refused to refund it, it was 'replace only', after arguing back and forth and eventually winning because I used a Canada Post Flex address, they agreed to giftcard me.  The Monitor was also DOA, I stupidly threw out the box after opening it before confirming it powered on. Newegg refused to RMA it without the box, and get this, they also refused to let me buy another one, and then ship this one back in that box! Because the box is unique to the serial number. My only option was to go to the manufacturer where I had to pay $110 to ship it.  I haven't bought anything from them since, and won't ever again. I emailed a complaint to their customer support and got ZERO response. They were completely unwilling to even make the slightest compromise or help out a customer in any way other than just "too fucking bad, deal with it, we already have your money".

If the manufacturers and distributors are an issue as to why they are strict on returns...the question shifts to why Amazon doesn't seem to have this problem with those same companies. People do receive stuff broken from Amazon, but they usually have no issue returning it. I can attest to this myself having received a broken refurb phone which I returned without issue. I get the feeling that if I had bought this phone from Newegg they would not have taken it back.  This is the problem that Newegg faces. I know people may abuse returns, my first job was with Walmart and people abused their returns all the time. But Walmart saw this as a cost of doing business, not a loss. Sam Walton himself would frequently joke about taking back broken items. There was a video with Walton joking about taking back a tennis racket that had been bashed, John McEnroe style. Give them a gift card, and they will go into the store, shop around, and probably buy a lot more stuff than just what the gift card had. That was the idea. There has to be a better way for Newegg to keep people happy and still make money.  Things may be more complicated today for online shopping, but again, that should be a cost of doing business. Amazon seems to have a grasp of this, and it is one of the reasons why Amazon is as huge as they are. They understand that if people are afraid to buy products because they may have trouble returning it, or returns are a hassle, people may not buy at all. Being sure I can return a product is one of the most important factors for me personally...that is why I bought my refurb phone from Amazon.  However, all of this could have been avoided if Newegg simply did its job in the first place and not allowed this defective board to go on sale. And it could have also been prevented if Newegg looked at this board's RMA history after GN returned it. So Newegg has managed to fail on 3 separate accounts.  1-Allowing this broken board to go on sale at all 2-Immediately blaming the customer for this damage 3-Failing to check the RMA history of this board, which proves the board was in fact damaged before it shipped to GN. The RMA sticker is on the board! Did they not even open the box???  Three different and very serious failures in a single transaction. I have to be honest here, I have a hard time believing this was NOT unintentional on some level. While I am sure Newegg has policies to keep this from happening, they obviously did not follow those policies. And I am positive that Newegg management has to be aware of these cases on some level, yet allows it to continue. How can Newegg management be so unaware of what is happening? This simply should not happen if somebody was doing their job at any point in time in the days before and after this sale happened. By playing willfully ignorant of these failings, I would argue that Newegg is indeed doing this intentionally.  So I do not see how this changes, even with this very high profile incident. This is systemic of their company, and would require a major overhaul. I can imagine that working their must be hard, and they probably have a skeleton crew of overworked staff trying to keep up with shipping all over the country. But that is not an excuse, in bad situations management should be on the floor trying to help when things get backed up, just like how managers at Walmart might run a cash register to help get lines down. So they need more staff, they need to fire management who allowed things to get to this state, and people need to be retrained. Nothing less is acceptable.

Newegg has been acting this way for YEARS.       I bought a IBuyPower PC from them back in 2015.  Decent deal, recommended by a friend.  Product is still up on the site, but has been out of stock for years.  I can offer the link if needed.    After the first big push of updates to Windows it ran, either the motherboard or some other vital component broke down and the PC basically was a paperweight.  Me being patient, I figured best bet was to contact Newegg (was still in my 30 day warranty period) and get an RMA.  So I got one, and had it shipped back to Newegg.  The rep I spoke with at Newegg even recommended that once they get the PC back to call in and ask to have the RMA expedited once it arrived.  All seemed at least going according to plan.  It took the entire week for their RMA to arrive and be acknowledged, and then nearly another entire week for them to complete their review of my RMA.  When I had called to ask that the process be expedited, I was told that they would not expedite.  The dashboard online at Newegg's website never updated giving me any indication of what the status of the replacement was, so I began calling their customer service number trying to get a status.  Called many times with no return calls regarding my issue.    I finally got through to a supervisor or two, and was told that my return was Rejected.  Apparently the PC I returned to them... the same one I received, hooked up and turned itself into a paperweight, and i put back in the same packaging to them, was not what they shipped me.  It didn't match their records.  Multiple representatives (politely) indicated I was trying to pull a fast one on them.   After what seemed like days of calls and dealing with multiple reps and supervisors, a supervisor told me that even though the serial number of my PC didn't match what they had on record, their records indicated it was sold by Newegg, and they would be willing  to accept my RMA. The rep indicated that it would be promptly shipped, but when I asked about what guarantees I would have that this replacement wouldn't fail like the first, he told me that it would be past the 30 day period from the original purchase, so they wouldn't be able to accept another return.   I got the replacement PC (after a bit more hassle of non-communication on their part), and set it up.  The next morning after setup, the system would not turn on.  Apparently a bad power supply.  After replacing that power supply at my own cost, the graphics card fan died shortly thereafter.  Two parts fail within days of setup... and while I am willing to accept that there could have been some fluke of a situation affecting the terminal... it is not likely at all.  The graphics card could have also been damaged by the power supply when it died.  Ended up replacing that on my own as well.  TLDR: Was sold a lemon, treated as a liar and a thief, not given the common courtesy of return calls, and then for my trouble shipped a SECOND lemon terminal with a faulty power supply and graphics card.

GREED is the ONLY ANSWER as to WHY. Don't take my word Google Intel, AMD, nVidia Year Profits and SEE with your own EYES. Intel used to be @62%, or more, Profit Margin. Do the math, in short if it's 62% that means a CPU costing 1,000 USD had a profit of 620 USD. Profit, BTW, is everything above actual cost of doing business so if Intel sold that same CPU costing 1,000 USD for 380 USD they'd still make profit just not 62% worth of profit. Newegg certainly used to be awesome.  But I knew it was inevitable it would become terrible.  A few years ago, Newegg became a publicly traded company.  That virtually guarantees that a company will become a total shitshow.  Due to how publicly traded companies work, every one is guaranteed to become a terrible place for employees and a terrible place for customers.  That is true even IF everyone who works at the company is 100% committed to making it a great place to work and a great place for their customers.  This is what happens:  Shortly after the company goes public, the CEO gets a phonecall from Goldman Sachs (or some other investment bank but its usually Goldman).  They say something like 'you are projected to grow 3.4% next quarter.  It needs to be 3.5%.'  The CEO knows the only way to get that extra .1% is to do something which would sabotage the long-term survival of the company and make it more terrible, like cutting staff, cutting wages, raising prices, cutting corners on QA, etc, so they push back.  The bank responds 'we, across our holdings and funds, hold a large percentage of your stock.  If you do NOT make those changes and get that extra .1%, we will dump your stock, causing it to crash and bankrupting your company.'  They are faced with a simple choice, either get nuked tomorrow, or die slowly over the years, getting slightly more terrible every 90 days, just to keep Goldman's knife off their neck.

I'm sorry.  Newegg has never been an awesome company.   They've always sucked.  They have this "in processing" bullshit, where they take longer than anyone else to "verify an order" which can take 1 to 3 days or who knows.  You pay for "rush processing" or express shipping, that doesn't matter.   Even in 2022 they don't show real time inventory, they don't show which warehouses they have stock so you can make an informed decision on what shipping speed to pay for.   Yes, I know they drop ship a huge amount of product from larger distributors, but those distributors have APIs that can integrate into their website.  Most normal mail-order companies, you can place an order before a shipping cutoff time (say 3pm EST) and they will ship 99% of orders out the same day.  You never know when NewEgg will get around to shipping an item.  I"ve paid for rush processing, next day shipping and gotten the product a week later.  I've chosen regular processing and free economy shipping and get it the next day.    Another thing they do is where they aren't an authorized distributor, so they will by from a bigger company like ASI, they will take the dealer rebates and subtract from the cost the CPU so they can sell a CPU below what your mom & pop shop who buys from authorized distributors are supposed to sell the chip for.  (loss leader)  but then they sell other items at not so great prices.   Sure, that's all legal, but it sucks for your smaller dealers that are trying to sell items at a (reasonable?) margin of like 5%- 12% because even if you sell at cost, NewEgg will always undercut them, and so someone wanting a custom build will see your CPU costs more than Newegg and won't even check the prices on remaining items.  They also buy gray market CPUs  (where CPUS are sold OEM in batches of 1000s to system integrators, NewEgg will buy excess inventory) also nothing illegal about this, but sucks because unlike authorized OEM CPUs (cpus sold without fan) there's no end-user warranty.   - - this is all before the scandal of refurbished/used/defective items "accidently" getting sold as "open box".  This is before the extremely bad luck that hundreds (thousands?) of people have where mysteriously their cpu socket pins get bent in shipping.   Or supposedly there's burn marks, or whatever excuses they have not to honor a warranty.   Not to mention the hoops you have to go through to get a refund  and the ridiculous times to wait for a replacement product and the total lack of quality customer service.
I can smell middle management putting pressure on lower management to "do something" to get rid of the piles of unfixed items so it stops effecting the bottom line of the return center(s). Lower management knowing they are stuck between a rock and a hard place is telling their employees (or the employees acting on their own) to just put it back on the shelf, hope for the best and washing their hands of it. Middle and upper management is happy the piles of non repairable product are gone sticking their heads in the sand not caring what is really going on.

Kudos to Eric for his willingness to go the extra mile. I see the Newegg issue as the same as computer brick & mortar retail went through in the early 2000's. They started cutting corners on returns which was an indication that their margins were getting squeezed and they needed to cut costs. It's no lie that many people abuse return policies for their own edification, its the largest reason that the DIY retail market died, with MicroCenter surviving.  It's also no lie that return volumes in the US between Thanksgiving until late January are off the charts as people decide that the gift was not what they wanted nor expected. Remember when DD-WRT first came out? I used to find mountains, I mean huge numbers or refurbished Linksys WRT-54's that were returned because they were bricked. Linksys hired a reflasher and they were reboxed and sold with no warranty. How many motherboards are returned to Newegg because some noob misflashed his $599 board?  Only Newegg can say, but these are the challenges resellers have when supporting the DIY market.

Information feedback RE i9 12900K Overheating  To the two most brilliant online experts?  I started a new build in JanuaryZ690 MSI Edge DDR4 Corsair Dom 3600 Corsair HX1000 Samsung 980Pro Corsair H115I water cooling  At stock Frequencies the cpu temps were hitting MAX all the time on any bench test. On the MSI board there are voltage ranges depending on the cooler specified. I dialed it back to a "Box" cooler limiting the wattage to 214W Still red lining. Bought a new cooler Corsair H150i Cappelix because I thought I was doing something wrong. Much better but still red lining under load. Sent CPU back to the shop for RMA. Just had RMA report back "Missing some Cores" Replaced!  Conclusion, Production line and testing not fit for new architecture. Anyone with overheating i9 12900K's send them back, it's not your brain bending, it's a CPU production problem.

1.  I wish companies would stop trying to minimize serious issues by framing them as "a very small number of (whatever)" in their borderline non-apologies.  It reminds of me is 2 a lot?  Dollars, no.  Men with knives blocking your way in a dark alley, yes.  When they minimize the issue it comes across as (because it is) disingenuous and dismissive. 2.  No, customer service is NOT the most important thing to you, profits clearly are.  Anyone with more than a week of business experience, and 2 brain cells to rub together, can look at your return/RMA policies and realize they are not customer friendly.  If customer service was your focus, situations where if you screw up the customer gets screwed over because they can't PROVE you were the one who screwed up wouldn't be possible.  Most of the time when these easily avoidable situations occur in a well established company, it's because some pencil pushing manager realized they could boost profit margins by 0.01% by cutting payroll hours in a "non critical" department that doesn't directly generate revenue (such as returns/RMA).  This leads to the department being understaffed and having to cut corners like eliminating inventory checks and redundancies that would catch initial mistakes.  When understaffed departments are rushed, they make mistakes.  Then they are rushed even more because having to deal with unhappy customers takes up even more of their time, leading to a vicious cycle of "shocking incompetence".  This is also of course discounting the possibility of malice, screwing over the customer intentionally because admitting fault or giving the benefit of the doubt when you know the mistake is probably on your end is more profitable (in the short term) than doing the right thing. 3.  Not really a shocking revelation, but I'm as always sick and tired of companies only (appearing to) addressing issues after the right people with a large enough online following (or news coverage) makes a stink about it.  If the upcoming interview doesn't reveal SPECIFICS on how they are fixing things in a meaningful and TRANSPARENT way, I'm officially done with Newegg forever.  Vague PR spin about "updating procedures" and the like aren't going to cut it.

A much better term would be a significant and unacceptable level of failures.  The "small number" is equal to a small problem and a refusal to actually recognize and address the issue.  The PR person handling the meeting is also a bad sign.  They will say all of the right things but were not part of the problem, have no first hand knowledge of the problem, and have no authority to make the appropriate meaningful changes to address the problem.  The PR person wants to fix the PR issue and not the actual issue.  The head of the RMA process, repairs, and exchanges should be involved.  They were the one that is ultimately responsible, should be aware of the issues, and directly in charge of fixing it.
Call me cynical, but I doubt any lasting change will happen at NewEgg.  I seriously doubt this sort of behavior was the result of a rogue manager in charge of returns and RMAs.  All of this stems from top-level management.  People at the top must know and are most likely complicit with what's been going on for the past 6 years.  Their goal was to burn the proverbial candle at both ends (the customers' goodwill and the company's reputation) to maximize their revenue to line their pockets.  They were going to keep this scam operation going for as long as they can, but now they got caught and called out by GN. 2016 in regards to chinese investment in the United States was right. Newegg Inc., one of the most popular online tech-related retailers, is now reportedly owned by chinese company Hangzhao Liaison Interactive Information Technology Co., Ltd. (Liaison Interactive).  After September's reports of a significant investment from the Chinese company on Newegg, which would allow the California-based company to "accelerate the pursuit of the company's strategic initiatives" and into high-growth markets such as eSports, VR (Virtual Reality) and AR (Augmented Reality), it now appears that the investment was much more than a simple capital infusion: it was a bid towards controlling an effective majority in the American company's shares.
